B004MWI2M0|5.0|This set, based on a scene from a Pirates of the Caribbean movie, is fun to build, has great play features, and is good value for the money.<br /><br />The build begins with putting together the four detailed minifigs. Next, you build the base of the mill, which is full of nice little details. Small pieces are used to give the walls a texture (a common feature in recent Lego sets); there are also stickers which are printed with rock overlays. Brown flat pieces are angled on the roof plates to give the impression of a ramshackle building. One side of the mill is a bell tower, which has a zip line -- a minifig can grab the handle of one end of a rope, and the other end is tied to a bell weight, which can drop, pulling the minifig upwards. The final part of the build is the mill-wheel itself. This is slightly tedious and has to be done precisely; the instruction book has a scale diagram that may help children do it properly. The end result is great, though -- the mill wheel sits on an indentation on the base, and there's a level that lets you pop it up so it rolls away freely. The wheel also spins, but you can balance swordfighting minifigs on top of it as long as you're careful to place them so they keep the wheel in equilibrium.<br /><br />Recommended for anyone who is buying multiple Pirates of the Caribbean sets, or appreciates clever Lego design.

B002KYHZLQ|5.0|I think Steven Erikson has done a great thing writing the books the way he has. I am on his second book in the series now, and they are extremely difficult to START. He throws you into a river and expects you to swim like a fish, which I think is very unrealistic for most casual readers. Once you get past the fast start, his books are extremely difficult to put down. He has done something I think is very cool by the way he engineered magic in his world. There are the different types but how people access their magic is very cool. Also, he puts a character into this book which has almost unlimited power, but he writes him very well. It's an easy trap for authors to just make their characters all powerful with no rules, and much harder to limit their powers, but he pulls it off excellently in this novel. 0547564651|5.0|*A full summary of this book is available here: [[ASIN:B00HMD4XLU An Executive Summary of Paul Tough's 'How Children Succeed: Grit, Curiosity, and the Hidden Power of Character']]<br /><br />The main argument: When it comes to a child's future success, the prevailing view recently has been that it depends, first and foremost, on mental skills like verbal ability, mathematical ability, and the ability to detect patterns-all of the skills, in short, that lead to a hefty IQ. However, recent evidence from a host of academic fields--from psychology, to economics, to education, to neuroscience-has revealed that there is in fact another ingredient that contributes to success even more so than a high IQ and impressive cognitive skills. This factor includes the non-cognitive qualities of perseverance, conscientiousness, optimism, curiosity and self-discipline-all of which can be included under the general category of `character'. In his new book How Children Succeed: Grit, Curiosity, and the Hidden Power of Character writer Paul Tough explores the science behind these findings, and also tracks several alternative schools, education programs and outreach projects that have tried to implement the lessons-as well as the successes and challenges that they have experienced.<br /><br />To begin with, Tough establishes how studies have now shown that while IQ and scores on standardized tests are certainly highly correlated with academic and future success, that non-cognitive characteristics actually predict success better than cognitive excellence. For instance, the psychologist Angela Duckworth has found that students' scores on self-discipline tests predict their GPA's better than their IQ scores. Likewise, it has been shown that the related characteristic of conscientiousness is even more predictive of a student's eventual success in college, and in their future earnings, than their scores on cognitive tests. Over and above this, it has also been found that both self-discipline and conscientiousness are highly correlated with all manner of positive outcomes, including in areas such as one's likelihood of abusing drugs and alcohol; getting in trouble with the law; maintaining healthy social relationships--including getting and staying married etc. And the good news character traits do not end here. Indeed, similar results have been found regarding the personality traits of perseverance (or grit), curiosity and optimism et al.<br /><br />Unfortunately, it has not been as clear just how we can cultivate these character traits in young people. Nevertheless, several promising avenues have been identified. To begin with, it has been shown that exposure to highly stressful and traumatic events in childhood can severely hamper the growth of character. However, it has also been shown that strong parental nurturance and attentiveness in response to these traumatic events can overcome the effect of the experiences themselves. In addition, the evidence is that the attentive and nurturing approach is effective even in the absence of traumatic events, as it is highly correlated with strong character development throughout the lifespan.<br /><br />While nurturance is certainly the most important factor early on, Tough argues that the cultivation of character during later childhood and adolescence requires somewhat of a different approach. Indeed, while the field is quite a bit more speculative here, it would appear that what is needed at this stage is for a young person to have the opportunity to take risks (some of which will no doubt result in failure), and to learn how to manage their failures in a constructive way. Success has been achieved using this approach in such programs as Elizabeth Spiegel's chess program at Intermediate School 318 in Brooklyn (while it remains a challenge at schools that cater to the wealthy, such as the Riverdale Country School in New York--on account of the fact that wealthy parents are increasingly shielding their children from failure).<br /><br />Beyond this, we find that results have also been achieved among some young people simply by steeping them in a culture of character, and by informing them of how certain character traits can lead them to greater success, and allowing their own ambition to take over from there. Indeed, this type of approach is practiced both at the KIPP family of schools, and in the OneGoal education program in Chicago. While the approach itself may not be a silver bullet, early indications are that it can indeed have a very positive effect.<br /><br />Tough's writing style is very readable, honest and unpretentious, and he does an excellent job of supporting the scientific evidence that he introduces with interesting and powerful anecdotes (indeed, many of these are enough to bring you to tears). This is a strong argument in favor of paying closer to attention to cultivating character in young people, both in our personal lives and in our public policy. A full summary of the book is available here: [[ASIN:B00HMD4XLU An Executive Summary of Paul Tough's 'How Children Succeed: Grit, Curiosity, and the Hidden Power of Character']]n from failure).<br /><br />Beyond this, we find that results have also been achieved among some young people simply by steeping them in a culture of character, and by informing them of how certain character traits can lead them to greater success, and allowing their own ambition to take over from there. B008CJ0JTI|5.0|Michael Douglas does one of the best jobs in his career playing the wealthy-but-jaded investment banker, Nicolas Van Orton, a man who tells his secretary, \\"You don't know about society, so you don't have the pleasure of avoiding it.\\" Divorced and living alone in his magnificent San Francisco mansion, Nicolas is enduring yet another birthday (his 48th, the same age his father was when he committed suicide) when he receives an unexpected visit from his younger brother, Conrad (a gleefully-going-for-broke Sean Penn), the black sheep of the family with a history of drug use. Conrad looks good, though, and he comes bearing an unusual birthday gift: a game.<br /><br />What follows is a chaotic, uncertain series of events that could be anything: social terrorism, a massive con, financial sabotage, or the world's most elaborate mind game. Run by a company called Consumer Recreation Services (or CRS), the game ends up infecting every aspect of Nicolas's life, from chance encounters with homeless men to the guy who reads the evening news. Nicolas's mind and life fray ever further as he struggles to figure out just what is going on. He is given an 800 number at the start of the game and told, \\"Do not call asking for the object of the game. Figuring that out is the object of the game.\\"<br /><br />Others have argued about plotholes in the script -- the game is unbelievably complex in both its planning and execution, and it relies on events adhering to a finely-tuned sense of timing and place -- but these concerns are dealt with deftly by the dialogue if you're paying attention. Ultimately, the point of the film -- what it takes to re-engage with life (let's just say it costs someone an exorbitant amount of money and someone else an exorbitant amount of pain) -- is so beautifully managed by Fincher's meticulous directing that whatever questions you might have about the reality of it all take a backseat to the intricate and masterful psychology of the whole thing. Maybe the point is that -- like the Bible says -- it is easier for a camel to enter the eye of a needle than it is for a rich man to enter heaven, even if heaven is just another word for whatever joy there is to find in life, be it family, friends, or love.<br /><br />Enjoy the ride, then watch it and enjoy it again. Each viewing, for me, reveals even more attention to detail and continues to be -- as it was when I first saw it -- a rewarding experience. Even still, as one character says, \\"I wish I could go back and do it again for the first time.\\" I can't do that with this film, but the message of the movie is that even when life feels like it has been lived to the end, maybe -- no matter how outrageous the cost -- there is a way to go back and start it over again.<br /><br />Film Buff Factoid: Pay close attention to the end of the film for a blink-and-you'll-miss-it cameo by Spike Jonze.is that -- like the Bible says -- it is easier for a camel to enter the eye of a needle than it is for a rich man to enter heaven, even if heaven is just another word for whatever joy there is to find in life, be it family, friends, or love.<br /><br />Enjoy the ride, then watch it and enjoy it again. Check us out at [...]<br /><br />With WWII around the corner and a looming Japanese occupation as its backdrop, author Paul French breathes life into the long forgotten true story of an unprecedented and officially unsolved murder of a wealthy British girl who died brutally at the hands of a butcher in 1937 Peking, China.<br /><br />I wanted to love this novel, I really did and part of me felt I was on the precipice of adoration, but I never fell. Love never came, only a minor fondness with a splash of disappointment. Not for lack of interest, in fact, the story was (at times) riveting, gruesome, sad, interesting and thrilling, but more often than not, it became tangled in a muddled retelling of Peking's colonial history.<br /><br />The author (French) paved an awkward path to the grand finale by weaving Peking's last days of colonialism as the Japanese and Generalissimo Chiang Kai-sheck neared invasion, with character back story and the quest to find a brutally murdered teen girls killer. My concentration broke with the constant intertwining of regional history while trying to keep up with the names of dictators, police chiefs, communist sympathizers, American ex-pats, murder suspects and English diplomats. It's not like I am a dummy, I just found the patterns and information scattered about distracting.<br /><br />Towards the end, all of a sudden the story  takes on a new life and I instantly became riveted. The focus of the story was placed solely on the murder and a fathers plight to find justice for his daughter (something that should have started about 100 hundred pages earlier). I started to appreciate where the path was leading only to find myself being pushed hastily toward the books finish. Revelations were thrown at me rather quickly and by the time I realized my ah-ha moment, French dragged me away kicking and screaming. Once more I felt short-changed.<br /><br />Look, I see what the author was going for. He wanted to capture what Erik Larson did flawlessly with The Devil in the White City, but fell short. Don't get me wrong, I finished it in a day and liked the murder investigation, it felt like reading a 48 Hours Mystery (thanks Lindsey for that analogy. It was perfect), but if it wasn't for this blog and the way the book kicked into high gear toward the last 75 pages, I would have given up and put it down about half way through.<br /><br />In no way am I discouraging anyone from reading it because it is well written and chock-full of deception, murder, corruption and Asiatic history, but I feel a warning is necessary for those who get bored easily with historical non-fiction. 0865478570|1.0|I was almost enjoying this book (only almost, because the subject is interesting, but the author will never use a word to describe something when five or six will do) until I read that \\"the signaling system of traffic lights is incapable of producing more than 'slow down,' 'stop,' 'get ready,' and 'go'. (The green-and-orange 'get ready' combination is used in the U.K., as a courtesy to drivers of ancient sports cars with gearshift sticks.)\\".<br /><br />This is a catastrophic sequence of factual errors and cultural crassness that undermines everything else in the book I had read so far. If the author can get this much wrong about something so simple (and easy to verify), what else in the book is, quite simply, invented? In a book that is, in many ways, about \\"truth\\" and how it can cross cultural boundaries (or not), that's quite an important question.<br /><br />Firstly, a traffic-light signaling system can do much more than that (and in many countries, does). Three differently-colored lights with--at a minimum--three states (on, off, regular flashing...) can communicate many more than four things (leaving aside the fact that the light can be combined with a symbol such as a direction-indicator). Anyone writing about the difference between languages and codes (which, in essence, is what's happening here) should really know enough about the math behind codes not to embarrass themselves like this. But that's just ignorance; what follows is worse.<br /><br />The UK's traffic light system has red (stop), red-and-amber (stop unless it is unsafe to do so) and green (go). Since relatively few of its drivers use \\"ancient sports cars\\", there is no need for the authorities who control such things to be specifically courteous to them. Of course, if you inhabit an imaginary world where all Brits speak like Terry-Thomas and drive open-topped Frazer Nash Le Mans Replicas (and where actual facts are irrelevant in a non-fiction book), this is unimportant.<br /><br />I was, despite the sometimes labored prose, engaged with the subject. Something as seemingly minor as this factual sloppiness and cultural insensitivity, however, makes me not trust anything I have read and it removes any incentive to continue reading. The book may have a great conclusion but I'll never know, due to the author breaching the all-important trust with the reader.

I don't want to spoil anything but I will say it's well worth the read, especially at 17 bucks or so, and better if you have a deeper knowledge of Batman's history. I knew there was more than one robin and I knew one of them was killed, but I didn't know who Jean Paul was going into the story. If you can ride it out you'll pick up bits and pieces alluding to the other robins and Jean Paul's history. It's not going to break you if you don't know every detail about Batman.<br /><br />I will say the book kind of slows down in the middle and it gets pretty unrealistic/cheesy at certain points, and there's definitely some questionable writing, but the action's great, Bane's story is especially cool, and like I said, it's well worth your money. If there's a comic book store nearby I'd suggest getting it from there; you'll spend a bit more but comic stores need all the help they can get.

B008D4AKYQ|5.0|Can I suggest there's a certain correlation between prog rock giants, Yes and with Big Big Train in the fact that each album of the former got better and better, especially from Fragile to Close to the Edge, from Tales From Topographic Oceans to Relayer onto Going For The One during those heady, special days when prog ruled. Thirty-five years or so later and another UK prog band is following along the same path in the sense it has been recognised that each album gets better and better. This isn't a band that rests on its laurels, and so it is in 2012 they have released English Electric (Part One) to a very favourable and appreciative audience who have looked forward to this album with eager anticipation. No mean feat given expectections were high from a very demanding and knowledgeable bunch of progheads. And the beauty of their music reaches out not only to those who live and breath prog but also to mainstream ears looking for good, well structured songs who maybe wish to have their rock album collection enriched further.<br /><br />If you haven't heard Big Big Train's music then you're in for a treat. The best way to describe their music is to maybe think about some of Genesis's earlier works recognised for their gentle arrangements creating moods of a romantic if not a better England with Victorian or Edwardian towns and rural villages over pastorale landscapes. Big Big Train have gone one step further and built a railway system over it. I even suspect they're Isambard Kingdom Brunel's long lost love child, but they do have their own style and sound as their previous catalogue demonstrates culminating in this new release of theirs. English Electric (Part One) starts off with feats of daring do with 'The First Rebreather,' a tale in itself of heroic diving deeds, and Big Big Train tell it well with musical flair and  a treat at the end of the long song with a collection of keyboards dueling it out between organ and synth/moog both. They're not as folksy as say Mostly Autumn lean towards sometimes, but they can head in that direction as with the next track, 'Uncle Jack' - a melody thanks to the introduction of banjo and upright bass mixed with the standard addition of keyboards, drum, guitar and vocals that stick like teflon in your mind, and one that will become a firm favourite due to the uplifting melody. But as in most prog rock there is a certain pervading tinge of melancholy about it; that sense of not so much days gone by but rather days passed by, much like in the works of Shakespeare and even Tolkien with England's pleasant greenery:<br /><br />\\"I sit beside the fire and think<br />of people long ago<br />and people who will see a world<br />that I shall never know\\"<br /> - JRR TOLKIEN (Lord Of The Rings)<br /><br />Something Genesis, and previous members of that band such as Anthony Phillips (The Geese and the Ghost, 1977) and Steve Hackett (Please Don't Touch!, 1978)have dwelled upon defined by their musical impressions. It has to some extent, with a touch of the Golden Industrial Age albeit followiing through to post World War II, become one of Big Big Train's signature references to their music.<br /><br />I've observed recent comments from fans of Big Big Train who have claimed their favouries from this album and I have to say I've changed my mind a few times from 'Winchester From St Giles' Hill' to 'Hedgerow' to 'A Boy In Darkness.' To be honest, there isn't a redundant song on here. David Longdon's vocals are spot on again, and I have looked forward to hearing him sing since the release of 'The Underfall Yard' and my favourite album (although it's recognised as an EP - that's EP in EPic in my books) 'Far Skies Deep Time.' Greg Spawton (guitars, keyboards, bass), prolific drummer, Nick D'Virgilio, Dave Gregory, guitar, mellotron, and Andy Poole on bass and keyboards once again show their musical ability with their own distinctive styles and methods that make up the Big Big Train sound we've come to love. What is exciting for a lot of us is how fresh they sound and what they bring to the prog table every time.<br /><br />In a nutshell, this is up there as the definitive prog rock album of 2012 and you won't be disappointed by any of the tracks on offer here. Each one is worthy of many replays to suit what ever mood or interlude you're after. Shades of the 70's but this band is making its own history in the 21st century. An amazing album by Big Big Train, and as someone who gets to hear and play a lot of prog for my radio show, highly recommended.

0439023513|3.0|**********spoilers*********<br /><br />If you've just finished the first two books in this series and loved them, it would be a good idea to dramatically lower your expectations for the third installment. You'll be a lot happier reader in the end.<br /><br />I read the entire trilogy within a two-week period and was absolutely enthralled with The Hunger Games and even more so, Catching Fire.<br /><br />After reading the first two novels carefully, as I would fine adult literature, I found myself zipping through Mockingjay as though I were reading the book version of the recent \\"Transformers\\" movies. If not for the need to discover how Suzanne Collins would ultimately bring Katniss and Peeta together, I might not have finished it.<br /><br />And yet, I found the story troubling, even several days after finishing it. I read the ending and epilogue multiple times, trying to figure out why I found it sound depressing and unsatisfying, despite the surface \\"happy ending.\\" Obviously, when a reader is still pondering a book several days after finishing it, the story succeeds on some level. But on another level, Mockingjay fails when it drops the theme of hope that had seemed to build throughout the series. The perfunctory epilogue did nothing to help. Both Katniss and Peeta appear to be just going through the motions of living - even though the author has given them everything to live for. If Collins intended otherwise, she failed with a great many readers.<br /><br />That leads to what I believe is the greatest flaw of the finale: Readers are \\"told\\" what happens, rather than shown. Thus, many readers will find that they just can't buy into the ending. Here's an example: In the last chapter, Katniss tells us, \\"Peeta and I grow back together.\\" Why did they have to \\"grow back together\\"? Wasn't it the love between the two that motivated much of what Katniss did? Didn't they already come together during the \\"Stay with me ... always\\" exchange in the Capital? If Collins thought that they needed to go through a process to reunite, then why not spend a few paragraphs showing how it happened?<br /><br />And then there's this contradiction: Back home in District 12, Katniss feels relief when she learns that Gale is no longer around, but after the return of Peeta, whom she was willing to die for, she goes into the forest and imagines Gale coming back. What? After all that, I'm supposed to believe she's so shallow she still wants both? Maybe the trilogy really was aimed at a teen audience after all.<br /><br />Many reviewers contend that those of us who disliked the ending were looking for a fairytale. That's not the case with this reader. Rather, I was looking for a final novel with the vivid imagery of the first two books and the continued growth of the two protagonists. Instead, Mockingjay was a hurried wrap-up that abandoned not only the hope but The Hunger Games' wonderfully real and well-rounded characters to a depressing end that seemed unworthy of their earlier determination and grit.<br /><br />It's still a good summer read. But closer inspection leaves it wanting.

Release date: Sept. 4, Hyperion Publishing<br />\\"Where did the time go?\\" \\"Time flies.\\" \\"I ran out of time.\\" \\"It's like time stood still.\\" They're just a few examples of the time-related phrases that we all use, words that imply is something that we can't control.<br />But, what if you could, would you want what comes with the responsibility?<br />Time - whether it's too or too little - and how it's handled by fallible humans is the subject of best-selling author Mitch Albom's latest , \\"The Time Keeper.\\" Just as with his other hits - \\"Tuesdays with Morrie,\\" \\"Have a Little Faith,\\" \\"One More Day\\" and \\"The Five People You Meet in Heaven,\\" his novel is a thought provoker, urging readers to find the depth in his simple, yet imaginative writings. He is the first man on earth to count hours, literally becoming Father Time. Rather than embracing time as a gift from God, Dor challenges Him when his wife dies.<br />Dor is banished to a cave when voices rise up from a pool as they ask, plead and demand more time. Centuries have passed, and Dor doesn't have the spiritual strength to take much more. God agrees to free him if he can help two people, a teenage girl named Sarah and an elderly man, Victor, embrace the true meaning of time. Armed with a magical hourglass, Dor travels through time into today's society, one dominated and dictated by time.<br /><br />He finds the lonely, heartbroken girl ready to kill herself. Victor is counting on his money to help him cheat death and live forever. One wants to cut her time short, while the other selfishly considers cryogenics so he can have another lifetime in the future. Dor's mission is to help them understand that time is a precious gift, one not to be squandered or manipulated. Instead, it should be embraced and lived fully. Can he save them in time?<br /><br />Albom's story is a reminder that we, as earthy beings, are not keepers of our time, but we are its celebrants. As such, it's up to each of us to decide how to spend whatever time we've been given. and not waste the precious gift. B008Y7N7JW|5.0|With author Suzanne Collins assisting in the screenwriting process, it's no surprise that the essence of the book is kept well intact. While avid fans of the book might argue for the inclusion of certain details or against the modification of some characters, the movie captures both the message and the mood of the book. The bleak rendering of District 12 is particularly effective, as is the excess of the Capitol. The vast majority of the casting is successful, particularly with Jennifer Lawrence as Katniss. (The stunning depth and range of her performance far outweigh any qualms critics may have regarding her less-than-emaciated physique.) Donald Sutherland portrays President Snow as calm, calculated, and thoroughly venemous, and Woody Harrelson imbues Haymitch with both cynicism and vulnerability. Elizabeth Banks' Effie is appropriately clueless, and Willow Shields creates a fragile Prim that is easily believable as the beloved younger sibling for whom Katniss would give her life.<br /><br />The frantic point-of-view camera shots let the viewer experience the whole ordeal as Katniss does, first-hand and chaotic. The explicit depiction of her injuries, along with those of Peeta and the other tributes assures that the brutality of the games is never minimized. To the same end, the killings themselves are given proper weight by being graphic without being gruesome. The transition from Rue's death to the rioting in District 13 is poignant and compelling; it is only later in the book that this is implied as a catalyst, but to see it in \\"real time\\" allows the viewer to feel the outrage as it escalates and also to anticipate the impending rebellion. Another useful modification is the creation of the control room for the games; in the book, we are only provided with Katniss's speculations as to the game-makers' methods, but the cold and clinical setting shown in the movie emphasizes the disturbing disconnect between the manufactured arena and the very real horrors ocurringwithin it.<br /><br />Finally, the final scene where President Snow pivots away from the control room screen showing the triumphant homecoming of the victors and marches up the stairs reminds the viewer that the dangers are far from over for Katniss and Peeta, and indeed, for the entire nation of Panem.<br /><br />The DVD bonus features are well worth watching, particularly the segment where Donald Sutherland explains how he sees President Snow and his essential role in the film. It's also interesting to see how many of the stunts are done by the main actors themselves.

Scientists have finally found a way to eradicate love, and the government demands that all its citizens receive the cure upon turning eighteen. With the cure, a person is able to live a life without pain; one that is safe, measured, predictable and happy. Lena Haloway has been looking forward to being cured but as she approaches her eighteenth birthday the unthinkable happens. She falls in love.<br /><br />I was not blown away by this book but neither was I completely disappointed. The idea of love being a disease that needs to be eradicated is intriguing and worked well in this book. I think the reason I found it so compelling is that, at some times it mirrored Islam's view on premarital relationships. Of course, Islam does not seek to eradicate love, however, it prohibits mingling with the opposite sex, other than what is needed for married and unmarried people. So the rules in DELIRIUM of no touching, kissing or excessive talking is the same for Muslims. However, despite the appeal, I have some issues with this book.<br /><br />I truly believe that DELIRIUM (400+ pages) could have been two thirds the size it was and not have taken away from the story. I agree with many other readers that the author's writing is wonderful and at times, almost poetic. However, it was overly descriptive for my taste and could have been cut down significantly. Also, I could say that there were exactly three moments where the book became exciting for about ten pages and then went back to trudging along.<br /><br />Another issue is that Lena seems to make decisions out of nowhere. In certain parts of the book, she is so determined that the government's rules are right and good, which is why I was confused when, the next second, she does the exact opposite to what she believed in. it didn't seem believable to me.<br /><br />I was disappointed to see that the author made lying okay, if it was for love. 0439023513|2.0|***Warning--major spoilers ahead...<br /><br />Upon finishing Mockingjay, I concluded two things: 1) this trilogy is a tragedy, and 2) Suzanne Collins believes executing a character arc is as simple as executing the character.  Here's why:<br /><br />Finnick: Shortly after his wedding, Finnick--one of the few characters who is actually somewhat developed throughout the story--is eaten by lizard monsters on a mission that was ultimately completely and utterly futile.  His sacrifice (if it can be called that) meant very little, if anything, to the story.<br /><br />Prim: The entire plot of the trilogy centered on the attempt to keep her alive, and she is killed in a random bombing at the end, as Katniss watches from a few feet away.  The entire plot of the story hinged on saving Prim from the evils of the age, and that battle is lost in a very grim way.  Katniss did not need her heart ripped out in this way to make the decision she made at the end.  This was an extra knife in the gut from an author who enjoyed bitter ends for key characters.<br /><br />Peeta: Though a large portion of the first book sets up Peeta and Katniss for a deep relationship, that relationship is largely ignored throughout the entire second and third books in favor of a shallow coexistence.  Peeta instead becomes a sort of zombie trying to kill Katniss, and never quite recovers; this leeches from Peeta his most valuable quality, which was his commitment to keeping Katniss alive.  Sure, he saves her at the end with his miraculous appearance at Katniss' attempted suicide, but without others' prior interventions, he would have already killed her on multiple occasions.  It seems as though nothing between his capture at the end of the second book and his appearance at the assassination at the end was either necessary or supportive of the plot.  He's tossed in at the end for a happily-ever-after that is anything but happy.  He deserved more and better--from the author as well as Katniss.<br /><br />Gale: This is perhaps the most tragic of treatments.  Gale's death would have preserved his character, but instead, Collins assassinates his character rather than taking his life.  In the last few pages, I was waiting and wondering how Katniss' first and oldest friend would make his return and begin the impossible task of making up for his role in Prim's death.  Instead, the reader is robbed of any closure beyond their insufficient parting at the Capitol.  The person who had vowed to protect Prim and had been there for Katniss--usually behind the scenes for the length of the trilogy--had failed in the most ironic of fashions and finally gave up on the Everdeens in the end.  He'd taken some job in another district, and the reader is left to assume that he and Katniss' relationship had died with Prim.  This isn't characteristic of Gale as we'd come to know him.  Had he died nobly at the end, perhaps to once again protect Prim, that would have stayed much truer to his character.  In my opinion, Collins certainly did not complete his character arc successfully, and traded plot for shock.<br /><br />Katniss: In the third book, it becomes more obvious with every page that Katniss' story is a full-on tragedy.  The reader loses a great deal of sympathy for her as she stops caring about those around her, and in her pusuit of killing Snow, she begins to kill civilians in the capitol.  3/4 of the way through Mockingjay, I found myself wondering why I was supposed to be rooting for Katniss anymore.  Her character degrades into little more than a tool of the revolution, without any kind of motivation beyond vengeance.  The entire push toward the capitol is a totally futile errand that accomplishes nothing but death; all those Katniss loves are either killed or broken along the way.  In the final pages, the reader is left with a smoldering ruin of a cast and story, with only a glimpse of future hope for something better.  Unfortunately, I didn't grow to care much for the namelessfaces of Panem's future; I cared for the characters of these three books.<br /><br />Make no mistake--I enjoyed the Hunger Games trilogy, and Mockingjay was an engrossing read that kept my attention.  I didn't expect hugs and rainbows at the end, but in any form of literature, the reader can only hope that the characters' existence had a good purpose and a satisfying end in the fictional world in which so much time has been spent.  Viewed as a larger story, I don't think Collins told a satisfying tale.  Whether or not that was her intention, I'm not sure, but as a reader, I felt her treatment (or perhaps more accurately, her disposal) of many of these characters was ultimately disappointing.

B004OEIL54|3.0|The SCREAM Blu-Ray is an overall solid HD upconvert of the original DVD: all the special features are transplanted, as well as original commentary. However, as upconverts go, it's pretty sweet, but the tradeoff is that the clarity of the transfer really accentuates the image problems (NONE of which have anything to do with the transfer itself).<br /><br />For starters, the film is finally presented in its original anamorphic aspect ratio, but this comes with a price. Considering that the film was shot on practical locations instead of sets, anamorphic may not have been the best format choice: the close quarters of real buildings really emphasize the \\"squeeze\\" effect at the edges of the frame in kitchens or narrow hallways. Original DP Mark Irwin was allegedly fired because of severe focus problems in dailies and I tend to believe it: there are glaring focus problems in the last 2 reels, and the wide-angle shots (10-12mm) have the depth of field of a medium (50mm) lens. I was surprised at how just HOW diverse the focus was throughout most of the shots and wondered why they didn't reshoot entire sequences because of it.<br /><br />The film's age also tends to show in the color representation. Cheap film stocks of the 90s used by indie filmmakers and Dimension films were prone to displaying dirt damage and luminance source with the same crispness as the picture so this can be a tad intrusive compared to single-generation master transfers of today, but at least they feel natural rather than a sign of poor maintenance. The color, however, is well-represented: it's not flat, faded, or washed, just a tad uninteresting.<br /><br />There IS a bit of pixellation in the blacks and darks, but fortunately they blend well with the natural film grain of the image, so it's very hard to tell which is which. And YES, I was looking for this deliberately. Some have complained of haloing towards the later part of the film but it didn't seem to bother me all that much considering that this is an HD transfer of a 16-year-old movie and not a full-blown JAWS/GODFATHER restoration.<br /><br />The soundtrack has already been listed in greater detail by others, but I agree: the sound is surprisingly stellar, even overbearing at times with how much detail is in the foley track.<br /><br />All in all, the Blu-Ray is a nice package for fans of the film or those nostalgic for the theatrical experience of 1996. For a film that hasn't undergone any restoration work, it's still in pretty good condition. It's not worth a $20 tag, but for $10-12 it's a welcome addition to the library.<br /><br />I paid $10 for it and don't regret it at all.is is an HD transfer of a 16-year-old movie and not a full-blown JAWS/GODFATHER restoration.<br /><br />The soundtrack has already been listed in greater detail by others, but I agree: the sound is surprisingly stellar, even overbearing at times with how much detail is in the foley track.<br /><br />All in all, the Blu-Ray is a nice package for fans of the film or those nostalgic for the theatrical experience of 1996. For a film that hasn't undergone any restoration work, it's still in pretty good condition. It's not worth a $20 tag, but for $10-12 it's a welcome addition to the library.<br /><br />I paid $10 for it and don't regret it at all.

Divine grace is the only explanation for the almost $700 million dollars his books-turned-movies have made worldwide.<br />  You think that by now Sparks would run out of ideas, but don't worry, love never gets old. In his newest novel, Safe Haven, Sparks finds a way to somehow keep the readers interested 16 books (and similar storylines) later.<br />  Mysterious newcomer Katie stars as the book's heroine. Her sudden arrival into the small town of Southport, North Carolina, sets the gossip mill turning. She doesn't try to make friends, she keeps to herself and she doesn't offer any details of her past life. As she begins to grow close with the caring widower Alex, the opportunity of a bright future presents itself--one free of fear, pain, and darkness. Although she wants her future to be with Alex, her past may come back to haunt her, bringing danger to her safe haven, Southport.<br />  During my long commute to work twice a day, I needed something to keep me interested during the drive. B006TF6WAM|4.0|What attracts me to books like The Unlikely Pilgrimage of Harold Fry is what I like to think of as their inherent quietness. Devoid of grand adventures and `epic-ness', these books tell stories of essentially the ordinary man and woman of the world. Harold and Maureen could be any old couple with a past. The issues they grapple with - loneliness, alienation from one another, coming to grips with their past actions - are recurring themes in all our lives.<br /><br />One fine day Harold Fry, a 65 year old retired salesman gets a letter from a co-worker he has not been in touch with in the last 20 years. On his way to post his reply, a fateful encounter plants in his mind the idea of delivering the letter himself, spurring his 500 miles journey across England from Kingsbridge to Berwick-upon-Tweed.<br /><br />The bulk of the book deals with Harold's journey, both literal and metaphorical. He re-visits memories he thought he'd long forgotten. He meets an entire cast of characters whose stories bring home to him the universality of the human experience. As Harold puts one foot in front of the other to reach Berwick-upon-Tweed, he begins to find an acceptance of his past - the one which was inflicted on him by his parents and the one which he thinks he inflicted on his son. Meanwhile, Harold's unlikely journey forces Maureen to step out of the world she's cocooned herself in, forcing her to see her husband in a new light.<br /><br />The prose is beautiful with some gorgeous descriptions of the English countryside. However, I did find some of Harold's encounters manufactured and a little out of the realm of the believable. A few of the `entourage' scenes seemed unnecessarily drawn-out - I wanted to tell Rachel Joyce that yes we understand that Harold is the quintessential English gentleman and that there is no need to belabor the point.<br /><br />While I enjoyed Harold's and Maureen's story I could not make myself love it the way I loved Major Pettigrew's Last Stand and The Guernsey Literary & Potato Peel Pie Society, two other books in the same vein. Perhaps this is because while hopeful, The Unlikely Pilgrimage of Harold Fry lacks the ebullience of the other two. (Though honesty compels me to add that this of course cannot be an indictment of the story itself - more than likely Miss Joyce intended it to be so). In any case if you don't mind quiet contemplative fares where the story is more character driven than plot driven you'll enjoy this one.

Nick, an out-of-work writer forced to buy a bar in order to make ends meet, becomes the police's prime suspect. 0061692042|3.0|\\"Canada\\" is the first fiction by Richard Ford that I've had the opportunity to read. For years, I'd been meaning to read him but never got around to it. When this novel was published, though, I read the dust jacket blurb and was intrigued enough to order it. Three things in particular from the summary intrigued me: 1) the two-staged plot, 2) Remlinger, and 3) the border setting. I'm a big fan of Cormac McCarthy and was drawn to read this novel by what appeared to be similarities to the latter's work--especially the Border Trilogy. Maybe authors have nothing to do with the blurbs that appear on the dust jackets of their books, in which case they shouldn't be to blame for falling short of the expectations created by them. But the experience of reading this novel turned out to be an interesting yet a disappointing one.<br /><br />First, it was interesting because of the way the whole story was announced in the novel's opening sentence. Rather than lessening the suspense, however, this technique actually heightened it. I knew what was going to happen, but finding out how it happened, watching it unfold, was pretty engrossing.<br /><br />Second, it was interesting because of the setting. Fiction is not too often set in this region of the country. Plus, as I said, the borderlands between Montana and Canada seemed like a northern version of McCarthy's Mexican novels. This was clearly a very deeply researched (or imagined) novel. The landscape of Montana and Saskatchewan (the prairies, the dilapidated small towns) were minutely and carefully described. It was enjoyable to inhabit that setting for 400-plus pages.<br /><br />I found a few of the characters interesting. In Part 1, Dell's parents, the way they were mismatched and their conflicts leading up to the bank robbery were at times fascinating. Dell's sister, Berner, was deeply developed, too (even before Part 3, where she has a central role). In Part 2, my favorite character was Charley Quarters--a mysterious employeeof Remlinger's who dyes his hair, wears makeup and has a disturbing side that's never fully revealed.<br /><br />Finally, the writing could at times be interesting. Often, especially in Part 1, the rhythm of the sentences took on a regional sound, almost like a southern drawl (Dell's father was born in Alabama). Often the language could be very striking, evocative of the past and the northern plains.<br /><br />Ultimately, however, I found the novel kind of disappointing. The reasons for this are as follows:<br /><br />The writing overall didn't rise above the level of blandness. It was mainly declaratory and factual, being preoccupied mainly with Dell's observations.<br /><br />Parts of the novel were over-written, especially in Part I surrounding the bank robbery, as if some paragraphs and even some entire chapters could have been cut out.<br /><br />The character of Dell I found to be irritating. As some other reviewers have said, he is completely passive in all the occurrences of his story. Passivity is akin to weakness, and it seemed at times that he should have exercised some agency, like his sister did. I realize that this aspect of his character is related to some of the novel's themes, but it was still annoying to watch him float along like a leaf on the current of his life's events. In fact, it is the lack of a solid relationship between Dell's character and the incidents of this novel that I find most confusing. Henry James famously asked (in his essay, \\"The Art of Fiction\\"), \\"What is character but the determination of incident? What is incident but the illustration of character?\\" Thinking of Ford's novel with this question in mind, I can only wonder in frustration what the incidents say about Dell and vice versa? Dell initiated nothing; he only reacted when other people moved. This means that his character is passive, obviously, but passiveness is not usually a likable trait in a person. And since there is no connection between Dell's character and theseevents, it makes the events themselves seem arbitrary. They could have been completely different without loss to the novel.<br /><br />Additionally, the relationship between the 3 parts of the novel seem arbitrary to me. I can suspend my disbelief, up to a point, that these two otherwise normal people could rob a bank (again, this is part of the novel's theme); but the events of Part 2 have absolutely no connection to that. True, Remlinger and Dell share some characteristics, but these are not enough to justify the nearly random nature of the book's main incidents.<br /><br />On top of this, the third and last part, which functions as a coda or epilogue, seems equally arbitrary, unconnected to anything that has happened before. It seemed oddly or forcedly tacked on (although, again, the character of Berner was intriguing, and I did feel a need to find out what happened to Dell and his family after the events of Parts 1 and 2).<br /><br />Finally, I was annoyed by the writing in Part 3 more than by that of previous sections. There was one grating error (Dell calls Conrad's story, \\"THE Heart of Darkness,\\" when there is actually no \\"The\\" in the title), there were silly cultural references (e.g., Dell and his sister go to Applebee's for lunch), the author's cultural prejudices are barely hidden in his description of Berner's late life, and there's a pointless endorsement of Obama that I found laughable. Does Ford really think this will resonate to readers in 50 years?

0545310601|4.0|Take a normal teenage girl with hormones raging, place her in enormously stressful and emotionally damaging situations and mix in a healthy dose of neurosis and you have an almost complete picture of Katniss Everdeen in the final book of this trilogy.<br /><br />This is a dark and at times disturbing end to the Hunger Games saga but begs to be read if only to find out how it all comes out (no spoilers here).  At times this book slows to a crawl as Katniss moans and whines about her lot in life and struggles with her symbolic role as the Mockingjay in the revolution of the districts against the Capitol.  Other times her incessant complaining simply wears the reader out and any sympathy and pathos the reader has developed for her is subtly replaced by a strong desire to smack the back of her head and yell, \\"snap out of it!\\"  If the author was going for the ultimate dysfunctional teenager, she knocked it out of the park.  And the wrong person dies at the end!<br /><br />However, Katniss perseveres through this macabre adventure and there are enough plot twists and surprises to prevent the reader from tossing the book out of the window before reaching the conclusion.  The worst of the three, The Mockingjay should be read in sequence since it is a more acceptable and appealing book than if read standalone.<br /><br />John E. Nevola - Author of The Last Jump - A Novel of World War II

B005CWRB44|2.0|First I want to say that I submitted this review on Fenix's website but they wouldn't post it.<br /><br />Although the E15 R2 is compact, bright and well-made I was extremely disappointed to find that the E15 technically has no physical On/Off switch.<br /><br />The Fenix documentation on their website refers to the light as having a \\"Twisty mode switch\\" which I mistakenly took to mean that there was a switch built into the lens/LED module which allowed you to turn it through four distinct positions.  In reality you're really screwing/unscrewing the light module from the barrel of the flashlight.  Whether the light is on or off, there's a bronze ring of exposed threads around the light.  This allows debris to get into the threads making the light more difficult to turn on and off.  I agree with other reviews which state that Fenix's choice of wording on their web site is deceptive in regards to the switch.<br /><br />The documentation also mentions one-handed operation to turn the light on and off but I found it very difficult to do this with one hand since the amount of twisting required to turn the light on/off was more than I could accomplish with one `twist'.  Changing the brightness levels also required a rapid twisting action that I couldn't perform with one hand.  Once the light is on the threads are still exposed so if you want to make sure the light is truly waterproof to IPX-8 you will probably need to screw down the LED module completely which also really has to be done with two hands.  If you are not fond of twisting the Lens/LED module to turn the light on and off then beware of any Fenix light that references a form of the word `twist' in describing the on/off switch as They have other lights with this same \\"Twisty mode switch\\" configuration.  Fenix's choice of wording on their web site is deceptive.<br /><br />One other small item, occasionally, when carrying the unit in my pocket, I will remove the light to find it on due to movement twisting the light on which is also not good considering a CR123 battery is a little over $6.00.<br /><br />If the E15 had a switch on the end of the barrel or a 4-position switch built into the LED module this would be a great little light.  If nothing else there should be a flange on the LED module that extends over the threads whether the light is on or off.  I just expected a little more for a $30.00 keychain flashlight.  If I had it to do all over again I would sacrifice the small size, spend the extra money and get something with a switch.

It's a story well told, and I'm sure it will accomplish what the author says he wants to accomplish -- convince some future teenage boy to become a SEAL. (In the meantime, it will convince many a middle-aged man that he SHOULD have become a SEAL!) It's exciting to get a first-person account. Makes you feel like you're having a beer with the author, but at the same time reminds you of the history involved.<br /><br />My only disappointment in this book -- and it's small, and on me rather than the author -- is that the story is so deeply personal. It's like looking at the raid through night vision glasses: what's visible is what's directly ahead of you. It's not yet the whole story of the raid, just one participant's experience of it. Owen acknowledges the contributions of the CIA analysts, the planners and the politicians, but finding out what some of their experiences were and including them was never part of his mission in writing the book. This is not quite the book that makes you feel satisfied you know the whole story. I'm looking forward to Mark Bowden's take on the raid.<br /><br />In the meantime, though, this is a good book to settle back with, enjoy, and marvel at how well the raid turned out. And maybe raise a glass to Owen and his fellow SEALS.
